The fact that the Court categorically prohibits life-without-parole sentences for juvenile nonhomicide offenders in the face of an overwhelming legislative majority <em>in favor</em> of leaving that sentencing option available under certain cases simply il-lustrates how far beyond any cognizable constitutional principle the Court has reached to ensure that its own sense of morality and retributive justice pre-empts that of the people and their representatives.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I agree with <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Stevens</small></span> that "[w]e learn, sometimes, from our mistakes."  <em>Ante</em>, at 1 (concurring opinion).  Perhaps one day the Court will learn from this one.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I respectfully dissent.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<a name="dissent2"></a>



<center><h2><p align="CENTER">TERRANCE JAMAR GRAHAM, PETITIONER <em>v.</em> FLORIDA</p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER">on writ of certiorari to the district court of appeal of florida, first district</p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[May 17, 2010]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span>, dissenting.</p>
<p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I join Parts I and III of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas'</small></span>s dissenting opinion.  I write separately to make two points.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>First</em>, the Court holds only that "for a juvenile offender who did not commit homicide the Eighth Amendment forbids the sentence of <em>life without parole</em>."  <em>Ante</em>, at 23-24 (emphasis added).  Nothing in the Court's opinion affects the imposition of a sentence to a term of years without the possibility of parole.  Indeed, petitioner conceded at oral argument that a sentence of as much as 40 years without the possibility of parole "probably" would be constitutional.  Tr. of Oral Arg. 6-7; see also <em>ante</em>, at 28, n.&nbsp;12 (T<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>homas</small></span>, J., dissenting).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Second</em>, the question whether petitioner's sentence violates the narrow, as-applied proportionality principle that applies to noncapital sentences is not properly before us in this case.  Although petitioner asserted an as-applied proportionality challenge to his sentence before the Florida courts, see 982 So.&nbsp;2d 43, 51-53 (Fla. App. 2008), he did not include an as-applied claim in his petition for certiorari or in his merits briefs before this Court.  Instead, petitioner argued for only a categorical rule banning the imposition of life without parole on <em>any</em> juvenile convicted of a nonhomicide offense.  Because petitioner abandoned his as-applied claim, I would not reach that issue.  See this Court's Rule 14.1(a); <em>Yee</em> v. <em>Escondido</em>, 503 U.&nbsp;S. 519, 534-538 (1992). </p>
<p></p><hr>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" href="#FRconcurrence2.*">*</a></strong><br><p><sup>*</sup>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> suggests that Graham has failed to preserve any challenge to his sentence based on the "narrow, as-applied proportionality principle."  <em>Post</em>, at 1 (dissenting opinion).  I disagree.  It is true that Graham asks us to declare, categorically, that no juvenile convicted of a nonhomicide offense may ever be subject to a sentence of life without parole.  But he claims that this rule is warranted under the narrow proportionality principle we set forth in <em>Solem </em>v. <em>Helm, </em>463 U.&nbsp;S. 277 (1983), <em>Harmelin</em> v. <em>Michigan,</em> 501 U.&nbsp;S. 957 (1991), and <em>Ewing</em> v. <em>California</em>, 538 U.&nbsp;S. 11 (2003).  Brief for Petitioner 30, 31, 54-64.  Insofar as he relies on that framework, I believe we may do so as well, even if our analysis results in a narrower holding than the categorical rule Graham seeks.  See also Reply Brief for Petitioner 15, n.&nbsp;8 ("[T]he Court could rule narrowly in this case and hold only that petitioner's sentence of life without parole was unconstitutionally disproportionate").</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNdissent1.1" href="#FRdissent1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span>'s concurrence<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small> </small></span>suggests that it is unnecessary to remark on the underlying question whether the Eighth Amendment requires proportionality in sentencing because "[n]either party here asks us to reexamine our precedents" requiring "proportionality between noncapital offenses and their corresponding punishments."  <em>Ante</em>, at 2 (opinion concurring in judgment).  I disagree.  Both the Court and the concurrence do more than apply existing noncapital proportionality precedents to the particulars of Graham's claim.  The Court radically departs from the framework those precedents establish by applying to a noncapital sentence the categorical proportionality review its prior decisions have reserved for death penalty cases alone.  See Part III, <em>infra</em>.<em>  </em>The concurrence, meanwhile, breathes new life into the case-by-case proportionality approach that previously governed noncapital cases, from which the Court has steadily, and wisely, retreated since <em>Solem</em> v. <em>Helm</em>, 463 U.&nbsp;S. 277 (1983).  See Part IV, <em>infra</em>.  In dissenting from both choices to expand proportionality review, I find it essential to reexamine the foundations on which that doctrine is built.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNdissent1.2" href="#FRdissent1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Courts and commentators interpreting this Court's decisions have reached this conclusion.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>United States </em>v. <em>Polk</em>, 546 F.&nbsp;3d 74, 76 (CA1 2008) ("[I]nstances of gross disproportionality [in noncapital cases] will be hen's-teeth rare"); Barkow, The Court of Life and Death: The Two Tracks of Constitutional Sentencing Law and the Case for Uniformity, 107 Mich. L. Rev. 1145, 1160 (2009) ("<em>Solem </em>now stands as an outlier"); Note, The Capital Punishment Exception: A Case for Constitutionalizing the Substantive Criminal Law, 104 Colum. L. Rev. 426, 445 (2004) (observing that outside of the capital context, "proportionality review has been virtually dormant"); Steiker &amp; Steiker, Opening a Window or Building a Wall?  The Effect of Eighth Amendment Death Penalty Law and Advocacy on Criminal Justice More Broadly, 11 U. Pa. J. Const. L. 155, 184 (2009) ("Eighth Amendment challenges to excessive incarceration [are] essentially non-starters").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNdissent1.3" href="#FRdissent1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court ignores entirely the threshold inquiry of whether subjecting juvenile offenders to adult penalties was one of the "modes or acts of punishment that had been considered cruel and unusual at the time that the Bill of Rights was adopted."  <em>Ford</em> v. <em>Wainwright</em>, 477 U. S. 399, 405 (1986). As the Court has noted in the past, however, the evidence is clear that, at the time of the Founding, "the common law set a rebuttable presumption of incapacity to commit any felony at the age of 14, and theoretically permitted [even] capital punishment to be imposed on a person as young as age 7."  <em>Stanford</em> v. <em>Kentucky</em>, 492 U.&nbsp;S. 361, 368 (1989) (citing 4 W. Blackstone, Commentaries *23-*24; 1 M. Hale, Pleas of the Crown 24-29 (1800)).  It thus seems exceedingly unlikely that the imposition of a life-without-parole sentence on a person of Graham's age would run afoul of those standards.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNdissent1.4" href="#FRdissent1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although the details of state laws vary extensively, they generally permit the transfer of a juvenile offender to adult court through one or more of the following mechanisms: (1) judicial waiver, in which the juvenile court has the authority to waive jurisdiction over the offender and transfer the case to adult court; (2) concurrent jurisdiction, in which adult and juvenile courts share jurisdiction over certain cases and the prosecutor has discretion to file in either court; or (3) statutory provisions that exclude juveniles who commit certain crimes from juvenile-court jurisdiction.  See Dept. of Justice, Juvenile Offenders and Victims: 1999 National Report 89, 104 (1999) (hereinafter 1999 DOJ National Report); Feld, Unmitigated Punishment: Adolescent Criminal Responsibility and LWOP Sentences, 10 J.&nbsp;Law &amp; Family Studies 11, 38-39 (2007).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNdissent1.5" href="#FRdissent1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Alaska entitles all offenders to parole, regardless of their crime.  Alaska Stat. 12.55.015(g) (2008).  The other seven States provide parole eligibility to all offenders, except those who commit certain homicide crimes.  Conn. Gen. Stat. 53a<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>-35</small></span>a (2009)<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>; H</small></span>aw. Rev. Stat. 706-656(1)-(2) (1993 and 2008 Supp. Pamphlet); Me. Rev. Stat. Ann., Tit. 17-a, 1251 (2006); Mass. Gen. Laws Ann., ch. 265, 2 (West 2008); N.&nbsp;J. Stat. Ann. 2C:11-3(b)(2)-(3) (West 2005); N.&nbsp;M. Stat. Ann. 31-18-14 (Supp. 2009); Vt. Stat. Ann., Tit. 13, 2303 (2009).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNdissent1.6" href="#FRdissent1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Colo. Rev. Stat. Ann. 18-1.3-401(4)(b) (2009) (authorizing mandatory life sentence with possibility for parole after 40 years for juveniles convicted of class 1 felonies); Kan. Stat. Ann. 21-4622, 4643 (2007); Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. 640.040 (West 2006); <em>Shepherd </em>v. <em>Commonwealth</em>, 251 S.&nbsp;W. 3d 309, 320-321 (Ky. 2008); Mont. Code Ann. 46-18-222(1) (2009); Tex. Penal Code Ann. 12.31 (West Supp. 2009).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNdissent1.7" href="#FRdissent1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although the Court previously has dismissed the relevance of the Uniform Code of Military Justice to its discernment of consensus, see <em>Kennedy </em>v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 554 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2008) (statement of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kennedy, J., </small></span>respecting denial of rehearing), juveniles who enlist in the military are nonetheless eligible for life-without-parole sentences if they commit certain nonhomicide crimes.  See 10 U. S. C. 505(a) (permitting enlistment at age 17), 856a, 920 (2006 ed., Supp. II).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNdissent1.8" href="#FRdissent1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Kennedy</em>, 554 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 12, 23) (prohibiting capital punishment for the rape of a child where only six States had enacted statutes authorizing the punishment since <em>Furman</em> v. <em>Georgia</em>, 408 U.&nbsp;S. 238 (1972) <em>(per curiam)</em>); <em>Roper</em> v. <em>Simmons</em>, 543 U.&nbsp;S. 551, 564, 568 (2005)<em> </em>(prohibiting capital punishment for offenders younger than 18 where 18 of 38 death-penalty States precluded imposition of the penalty on persons under 18 and the remaining 12 States did not permit capital punishment at all); <em>Atkins</em> v. <em>Virginia</em>, 536 U.&nbsp;S. 304, 314-315 (2002) (prohibiting capital punishment of mentally retarded persons where 18 of 38 death-penalty States precluded imposition of the penalty on such persons and the remaining States did not authorize capital punishment at all);<em> Thompson</em> v. <em>Oklahoma, </em>487 U.&nbsp;S. 815, 826, 829 (1988) (plurality opinion)<em> </em>(prohibiting capital punishment of offenders under 16 where 18 of 36 death-penalty States precluded imposition of the penalty on such persons and the remaining States did not permit capital punishment at all);<em> Enmund</em> v. <em>Florida, </em>458 U.&nbsp;S. 782, 789 (1982) (prohibiting capital punishment for felony murder without proof of intent to kill where eight States allowed the punishment without proof of that element); <em>Coker</em> v. <em>Georgia</em>, 433 U.&nbsp;S. 584, 593 (1977)<em> </em>(holding capital punishment for the rape of a woman unconstitutional where "[a]t no time in the last 50 years have a majority of the States authorized death as a punishment for rape").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNdissent1.9" href="#FRdissent1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;I say "recent memory" because the research relied upon by the Court provides a headcount of juvenile nonhomicide offenders presently incarcerated in this country, but does not provide more specific information about all of the offenders, such as the dates on which they were convicted.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNdissent1.10" href="#FRdissent1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Because existing legislation plainly suffices to refute any consensus against this sentencing practice, I assume the accuracy of the Court's evidence regarding the frequency with which this sentence has been imposed.  But I would be remiss if I did not mention two points about the Court's figures.  First, it seems odd that the Court counts only those juveniles sentenced to life without parole and excludes from its analysis all juveniles sentenced to lengthy term-of-years sentences (<em>e.g.</em>, 70 or 80 years' imprisonment).  It is difficult to argue that a judge or jury imposing such a long sentence--which effectively denies the offender any material opportunity for parole--would express moral outrage at a life-without-parole sentence.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Second, if objective indicia of consensus were truly important to the Court's analysis, the statistical information presently available would be woefully inadequate to form the basis of an Eighth Amendment rule that can be revoked only by constitutional amendment.  The only evidence submitted to this Court regarding the frequency of this sentence's imposition was a single study completed after this Court granted certiorari in this case.  See P. Annino, D. Rasmussen, &amp; C. Rice, Juvenile Life Without Parole for Non-Homicide Offenses: Florida Compared to Nation 2 (Sept. 14, 2009).  Although I have no reason to question the professionalism with which this study was conducted, the study itself acknowledges that it was incomplete and the first of its kind.  See <em>id.</em>, at 1.  The Court's questionable decision to "complete" the study on its own does not materially increase its reliability.  For one thing, by finishing the study itself, the Court prohibits the parties from ever disputing its findings.  Complicating matters further, the original study sometimes relied on third-party data rather than data from the States themselves, see <em>ibid.;</em> the study has never been peer reviewed; and specific data on all 129 offenders (age, date of conviction, crime of conviction, etc.), have not been collected, making verification of the Court's headcount impossible.  The Court inexplicably blames Florida for all of this.  See <em>ante</em>, at 12.  But as already noted, it is not Florida's burden to collect data to prove a national consensus in favor of this sentencing practice, but Graham's "heavy burden" to prove a consensus <em>against</em> it.  See <em>supra</em>, at 16.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNdissent1.11" href="#FRdissent1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;I confine to a footnote the Court's discussion of foreign laws and sentencing practices because past opinions explain at length why such factors are irrelevant to the meaning of our Constitution or the Court's discernment of any longstanding tradition in <em>this</em> Nation.  See <em>Atkins</em>, 536 U.&nbsp;S., at 324-325 (Rehnquist, C.&nbsp;J., dissenting).  Here, two points suffice.  First, despite the Court's attempt to count the actual number of juvenile nonhomicide offenders serving life-without-parole sentences in other nations (a task even more challenging than counting them within our borders), the <em>laws</em> of other countries permit juvenile life-without-parole sentences,  see Child Rights Information, Network, C. de la Vega, M. Montesano, &amp; A. Solter, Human Rights Advocates, Statement on Juvenile Sentencing to Human Rights Council, 10th Sess. (Nov. 3, 2009) ("Eleven countries have laws with the potential to permit the sentencing of child offenders to life without the possibility of release"), online at http://www.crin.org/resources/infoDetail.asp?ID=19806) (as visited May 14, 2010, and available in Clerk of Court's case file)).  Second, present legislation notwithstanding, democracies around the world remain free to adopt life-without-parole sentences for juvenile offenders tomorrow if they see fit.  Starting today, ours can count itself among the few in which judicial decree prevents voters from making that choice.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNdissent1.12" href="#FRdissent1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;It bears noting that Colorado, one of the five States that prohibit life-without-parole sentences for juvenile nonhomicide offenders, permits such offenders to be sentenced to mandatory terms of imprisonment for up to 40 years.  Colo. Rev. Stat. 18-1.3-401(4)(b) (2009).  In light of the volume of state and federal legislation that presently <em>permits</em> life-without-parole sentences for juvenile nonhomicide offenders, it would be impossible to argue that there is any objective evidence of agreement that a juvenile is constitutionally entitled to a parole hearing any sooner than 40 years after conviction.  See Tr. of Oral Arg. 6-7 (counsel for Graham, stating that, "[o]ur position is that it should be left up to the States to decide.  We think that the ... Colorado provision would probably be constitutional").</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>

  var map = {

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

setTrackerCookie('fl_last_page_view_id', _satellite.getVar('page_view_id') );
</script><script>_satellite["_runScript5"](function(event, target, Promise) {
window.addEventListener("load", function(event) {
  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      const forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      _satellite.logger.info('forms',forms);
      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    _satellite.logger.info('links',links);
    links.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        const goTo = link.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        let source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';
        if (!source) {
          if (link.closest('.cta-public')) {
            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {
          sidebarLawyerSearch.addEventListener('click', function(event) {
            if (event.target.getAttribute('href') && event.target.getAttribute('href').includes('lawyers.findlaw.com')) {
              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {
        link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Top Counties";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Legal Issue";
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        });
      });
    }
    
    //data-search-location
    const linksWithSearchLocation = document.querySelectorAll('[data-search-location] a, a[data-search-location]');
    _satellite.logger.info('linksWithSearchLocation',linksWithSearchLocation);
    linksWithSearchLocation.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        var goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        var source = e.currentTarget.dataset.searchLocation || e.currentTarget.dataset.closest("[data-search-location]").dataset.searchLocation;
        _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    });

  } catch (exp) {
    _satellite.logger.log("Issue with 'Search Source' Rule");
  }
  
});
